<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Dissecting the genome of star fruit ( Averrhoa carambola L.)"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-06-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Averrhoa carambola is commonly known as star fruit because of its peculiar shape, and its fruit is a rich source of minerals and vitamins. It is also used in traditional medicines in countries such as India, China, the Philippines, and Brazil for treating various ailments, including fever, diarrhea, vomiting, and skin disease. Here, we present the first draft genome of the Oxalidaceae family, with an assembled genome size of 470.51&#8201;Mb. In total, 24,726 protein-coding genes were identified, and 16,490 genes were annotated using various well-known databases. The phylogenomic analysis confirmed the evolutionary position of the Oxalidaceae family. Based on the gene functional annotations, we also identified enzymes that may be involved in important nutritional pathways in the star fruit genome. Overall, the data from this first sequenced genome in the Oxalidaceae family provide an essential resource for nutritional, medicinal, and cultivational studies of the economically important star-fruit plant."/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Averrhoa carambola</i> is commonly known as star fruit because of its peculiar shape, and its fruit is a rich source of minerals and vitamins. It is also used in traditional medicines in countries such as India, China, the Philippines, and Brazil for treating various ailments, including fever, diarrhea, vomiting, and skin disease. Here, we present the first draft genome of the Oxalidaceae family, with an assembled genome size of 470.51âMb. In total, 24,726 protein-coding genes were identified, and 16,490 genes were annotated using various well-known databases. The phylogenomic analysis confirmed the evolutionary position of the Oxalidaceae family. Based on the gene functional annotations, we also identified enzymes that may be involved in important nutritional pathways in the star fruit genome. Overall, the data from this first sequenced genome in the Oxalidaceae family provide an essential resource for nutritional, medicinal, and cultivational studies of the economically important star-fruit plant.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The star-fruit plant (<i>Averrhoa carambola</i> L.), a member of the Oxalidaceae family, is a medium-sized tree that is distinguished by its unique, attractive star-shaped fruit (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">1</a>). <i>A. carambola</i> is widely distributed around the world, especially in tropical countries such as India, Malaysia, Indonesia, and the Philippines, and is considered an important species; thus, it is extensively cultivated in Southeast Asia and Malaysia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muthu, N., Lee, S. Y., Phua, K. K. &amp; Bhore, S. J. Nutritional, medicinal and toxicological attributes of star-fruits (Averrhoa carambola L.): a review. Bioinformation 12, 420â424 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d66053e620">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Khoo, H. et al. A review on underutilized tropical fruits in Malaysia. Guangxi Agric. Sci. 41, 698â702 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d66053e623">2</a></sup>. It is also a popular fruit in the markets of the United States, Australia, and the South Pacific Islands<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ray, P. K. Breeding Tropical and Subtropical Fruits, XVI. 338 (Springer-Verlag, Berlin, 2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d66053e627">3</a></sup>. Star fruits have a unique taste, with a slightly tart, acidic (in smaller fruits) or sweet, mild flavor (in large fruits). Star fruit is a good source of various minerals and vitamins and is rich in natural antioxidants such as vitamin C and gallic acid. Moreover, the presence of high amounts of fiber in these fruits aids in absorbing glucose, retarding glucose diffusion into the bloodstream and controlling the blood glucose concentration.</p><p>In addition to its use as a food source, star fruit is utilized as an herb in India, Brazil, and Malaysia and is widely used in traditional Chinese medicine preparations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, S.-C., Wu, S.-H. &amp; Chau, C.-F. Improvement of the hypocholesterolemic activities of two common fruit fibers by micronization processing. J. Agric. Food Chem. 57, 5610â5614 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d66053e634">4</a></sup> as a remedy for fever, asthma, headache, and skin diseases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Cabrini, D. A. et al. Analysis of the potential topical anti-inflammatory activity of Averrhoa carambola L. in mice. Evid. Based Complement. Altern. Med. 2011, 908059â908059 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d66053e638">5</a></sup>. Several studies have demonstrated the presence of various phytochemicals, such as saponins, flavonoids, alkaloids, and tannins, in the leaves, fruits, and roots of star-fruit plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Shui, G. &amp; Leong, L. P. Analysis of polyphenolic antioxidants in star fruit using liquid chromatography and mass spectrometry. J. Chromatogr. A 1022, 67â75 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d66053e642">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Annegowda, H. V., Bhat, R., Min-Tze, L., Karim, A. A. &amp; Mansor, S. M. Influence of sonication treatments and extraction solvents on the phenolics and antioxidants in star fruits. J. Food Sci. Technol. 49, 510â514 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d66053e645">7</a></sup>; these compounds are known to confer antioxidant and specific healing properties. A study by Cabrini et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Cabrini, D. A. et al. Analysis of the potential topical anti-inflammatory activity of Averrhoa carambola L. in mice. Evid. Based Complement. Altern. Med. 2011, 908059â908059 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d66053e649">5</a></sup> indicated that the ethanolic extract from <i>A. carambola</i> is highly effective in minimizing the symptoms of ear swelling (edema) and cellular migration in mice. A flavonoid compound (apigenin-6-C-Î²-fucopyranoside) isolated from <i>A. carambola</i> leaves showed anti-hyperglycemic action in rats, and might show potential for use in the treatment and prevention of diabetes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cazarolli, L. H. et al. Anti-hyperglycemic action of apigenin-6-C-Î²-fucopyranoside from Averrhoa carambola. Fitoterapia 83, 1176â1183 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d66053e660">8</a></sup>. Moreover, 2-dodecyl-6-methoxycyclohexa-2,5-diene-1,4-dione (DMDD) extracted from the roots of <i>A. carambola</i> exhibits potential benefits in the treatment of obesity, insulin resistance, and memory deficits in Alzheimerâs disease<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Suluvoy, J. K., Sakthivel, K. M., Guruvayoorappan, C. &amp; Berlin Grace, V. M. Protective effect of Averrhoa bilimbi L. fruit extract on ulcerative colitis in wistar rats via regulation of inflammatory mediators and cytokines. Biomed. Pharmacother. 91, 1113â1121 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66053e667">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wei, X. et al. Protective effects of 2-dodecyl-6-methoxycyclohexa-2,5-diene-1,4-dione isolated from Averrhoa carambola L. (Oxalidaceae) roots on neuron apoptosis and memory deficits in Alzheimerâs disease. Cell. Physiol. Biochem. 49, 1105â1114 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d66053e670">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Although <i>A. carambola</i> plays very significant roles in traditional medicine applications, there are very limited studies on <i>A. carambola</i> at the genetic level, mainly due to a lack of genome information. Therefore, filling this genomic gap will help researchers to fully explore and understand this agriculturally important plant. As a part of the 10KP project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Cheng, S. et al. 10KP: a phylodiverse genome sequencing plan. Gigascience 7, giy013 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d66053e683">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Liu, H. et al. Molecular digitization of a botanical garden: high-depth whole genome sequencing of 689 vascular plant species from the Ruili Botanical Garden. GigaScience 8, 1â9 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d66053e686">12</a></sup>, the draft genome of <i>A. carambola</i> collected from the Ruili Botanical Garden in Yunnan, China, was assembled in this study using an advanced 10X genomics technique to further elucidate the evolution of the Oxalidaceae family. A fully annotated genome of <i>A. carambola</i> will serve as a foundation for pharmaceutical applications of the species and the improvement of breeding strategies for the star-fruit plant.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and evaluation</h3><p>Based on k-mer analysis, a total of 35,655,285,391 k-mers were used, with peak coverage of 75. The <i>A. carambola</i> genome was estimated to be ~475âMb in size (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">2</a>). To perform genome assembly, a total of 156âGb of clean reads were utilized by Supernova v2.1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Weisenfeld, N. I., Kumar, V., Shah, P., Church, D. M. &amp; Jaffe, D. B. Direct determination of diploid genome sequences. Genome Res. 27, 757â767 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d66053e714">13</a></sup>. The final assembly contained 69,402 scaffold sequences, with an N50 of 2.76âMb, and 78,313 contig sequences, with an N50 of 44.84âKb, for a total assembly size of 470.51âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Tab1">1</a>). Completeness assessment was performed using Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) version 3.0.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 543â548 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d66053e721">14</a></sup> with Embryophyta odb9. The results showed that 1327 (92.20%) of the expected 1440 conserved plant orthologs were detected as complete (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">1</a>). To further evaluate the completeness of the assembled genome, we performed short read mapping using clean raw data with BWA-MEM software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Vasimuddin, M. et al. Efficient Architecture-Aware Acceleration of BWA-MEM for Multicore Systems. IEEE International Parallel and Distributed Processing Symposium (IPDPS), Rio de Janeiro, Brazil, 314â324 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d66053e729">15</a></sup>. In total, 943,278,896 (99.12%) reads could be mapped to the genome, and 88.13% of them were properly paired (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>A total of 68.15% of the assembled <i>A. carambola</i> genome was composed of repetitive elements (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">3</a>). Among these repetitive sequences, LTRs were the most abundant, accounting for 61.64% of the genome. DNA class repeat elements represented 4.19% of the genome; LINE and SINE classes accounted for 0.28% and 0.016% of the assembled genome, respectively. For gene prediction, we combined homology-based and de novo-based approaches and obtained a non-redundant set of 24,726 gene models with 4.11 exons per gene on average. The gene length was 3457âbp on average, while the average exon and intron lengths were 215 and 827âbp, respectively. The gene model statistical data compared with seven other closely related species are shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">3</a>. To evaluate the completeness of the gene models for <i>A. carambola</i>, we used BUSCO with Embryophyta odb9. A total of 1281 (88.9%) complete orthologs were detected from the predicted star fruit gene sets (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>Functions were assigned to 16,490 (66.69%) genes. These protein-coding genes were then subjected to further exploration against the KEGG, NR, and COG protein sequence databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tatusov, R. L., Galperin, M. Y., Natale, D. A. &amp; Koonin, E. V. The COG database: a tool for genome-scale analysis of protein functions and evolution. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 33â36 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1106">16</a></sup>, in addition to the SwissProt and TrEMBL databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45â48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1110">17</a></sup>, and InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116âW120 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1114">18</a></sup> was finally used to identify domains and motifs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">5</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">4</a>). Noncoding RNA genes in the assembled genome were also annotated. We predicted 759 tRNA, 1341 rRNA, 90 microRNA (miRNA), and 2039 small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes in the assembled genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>Since star fruit is an important cultivated plant, the identification of disease resistance genes was one of the focuses of our study. Nucleotide-binding site (NBS) genes play an important role in pathogen defense and the cell cycle. We identified a total of 80 non-redundant NBS-encoding orthologous genes in the star fruit genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">7</a>). Among these genes, TIR (encoding the toll interleukin receptor) motif was found to be significantly smaller than in other eudicot plants, except for cocoa. Unlike other plants, the leucine-rich repeat (LRR) motif was not the most or second most common motif in the NBS gene family in star fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1134">19</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome evolution</h3><p>The characterization of the star fruit genome can provide necessary data for further analyzing the evolutionary history of Oxalidaceae. A Î³ whole-genome triplication event affected over 75% of extant angiosperms and was associated with the early diversification of the core eudicots. To investigate evolutionary events at the genomic level in star fruit, we identified 1134 paralogous gene families on the basis of the 24,726 gene models. The synonymous substitution rates (Ks) in the duplicated genes (Ksâ=â1.9) suggested that an ancient Î³ event occurred in star fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig1">1a</a>). Furthermore, we assessed the intergenomic collinearity among the <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Swarbreck, D. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): gene structure and function annotation. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D1009âD1014 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1151">20</a></sup>, poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1155">21</a></sup>, and grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1159">22</a></sup> genomes and identified relationships among star fruit orthologues. The mean Ks values from the one-to-one orthology analysis of star fruit in relation to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, poplar, and grape were 1.8, 1.0, and 1.2, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig1">1bâd</a>). The results confirmed the shared ancient whole-genome duplications (WGDs) event between the four species. Moreover, we generated whole-genome syntenic dotplots of star fruit based on the Ks value (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig1">1e</a>). Over 50% of the syntenic blocks shared a Ks rate between 1.0 and 2.0, and only ~10% of the gene pairs exhibited a Ks below 1.0, which indicated that no recent WGDs have occurred in the star fruit genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The analysis of A. carambola whole-genome duplications."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: The analysis of <i>A. carambola</i> whole<i>-</i>genome duplications.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="511" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The distribution of synonymous substitution rate (Ks) distance values observed for <b>a</b> <i>A. carambola-</i>paralog, <b>b</b> <i>A. carambolaâA. thaliana</i> ortholog, <b>c</b> <i>A. carambolaâV. vinifera</i> ortholog, and <b>d</b> <i>A. carambolaâP. trichocarpa</i> ortholog. <b>e</b> The <i>A. carambola</i> paralog colinear blocks are colored according to Ks values.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene family analysis and phylogenetic tree</h3><p>We performed <i>A. carambola</i> gene family analysis using OrthoMCL software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1242">23</a></sup> with protein and nucleotide sequences from <i>A. carambola</i> and 10 other plant species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>F. sylvatica</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>P. granatum</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>) based on an all-versus-all BLASTP alignment with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1eâ05. The 24,726 predicted protein-coding genes in <i>A. carambola</i> were assigned to 9731 gene families consisting of 15,301 genes, while 9425 genes were not organized into groups and were unique to <i>A. carambola</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">8</a>, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig2">2b</a>). In total, 163 single-copy orthologs corresponding to the 11 species were extracted from the clusters and used to construct the phylogenetic tree. The constructed tree topology supported the APG IV<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1297">24</a></sup> system in which Oxalidales (<i>A. carambola</i>) and Malpighiales (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>) are sister clades that belong to the same cluster (rosids). Based on the phylogenetic tree, <i>A. carambola</i> was estimated to have separated from <i>P. trichocarpa, V. vinifera</i>, and <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i> approximately 94.5, 110.2, and 126.3 Mya, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Gene family analysis and phylogenetic tree construction."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Gene family analysis and phylogenetic tree construction.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="278" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p> <b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree showing the sizes of expanded and contracted gene families. Pungra: <i>P. granatum;</i> Poptri: <i>P. trichocarpa;</i> Avecar: <i>A. carambola;</i> Thecac: <i>T. cacao;</i> Aratha: <i>A. thaliana;</i> Citsin: <i>C. sinensis;</i> Fagsyl: <i>F. sylvatica;</i> Maldom: <i>M. domestica;</i> Glymax: <i>G. max</i>; Vitvin: <i>V. vinifera</i>; Kalfed: <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>. <b>b</b> Venn diagram of the number of shared gene families within <i>A. carambola</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also analyzed the expansion and contraction of the gene families between species using CAFÃ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1396">25</a></sup>. The results showed that 888 gene families were substantially expanded and that 15,724 gene families were contracted in <i>A. carambola</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig2">2a</a>). In total, 2916 and 6057 genes identified in <i>A. carambola</i> came from expanded and contracted families, respectively, where contraction was approximately two times more common than expansion.</p><p>Gene ontology (GO) and KEGG functional enrichment analyses were subsequently performed for all expanded gene families. The KEGG pathway enrichment analysis results are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Tab2">2</a>, and the GO-enrichment results are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">9</a>. In a previous study, several flavonoids were isolated from the fresh fruit of <i>A. carambola</i>, which are known to reduce harmful inflammation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jia, X., Xie, H., Jiang, Y. &amp; Wei, X. Flavonoids isolated from the fresh sweet fruit of Averrhoa carambola, commonly known as star fruit. Phytochemistry 153, 156â162 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1421">26</a></sup>. In our study, the flavonoid biosynthesis pathway was found to be significantly enriched among the expanded families. Terpenoids are yet another important type of compound that has been isolated from star fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Moresco, H. H., Queiroz, G. S., Pizzolatti, M. G. &amp; Brighente, I. M. Chemical constituents and evaluation of the toxic and antioxidant activities of Averrhoa carambola leaves. Braz. J. Pharmacogn. 22, 319â324 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1425">27</a></sup> and has been proven to exhibit anti-inflammatory activities. <i>A. carambola</i> likely synthesizes terpenoids via the diterpenoid biosynthesis pathway.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Enriched KEGG pathways of unique genes of <i>A. carambola</i> showing expansion.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genes specifically involved in star fruit nutrition pathways</h3><p>Star fruit is an excellent source of various minerals and vitamins, especially natural antioxidants, such as <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-ascorbic acid (vitamin C) and riboflavin (vitamin B<sub>2</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muthu, N., Lee, S. Y., Phua, K. K. &amp; Bhore, S. J. Nutritional, medicinal and toxicological attributes of star-fruits (Averrhoa carambola L.): a review. Bioinformation 12, 420â424 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1901">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jia, X., Xie, H., Jiang, Y. &amp; Wei, X. Flavonoids isolated from the fresh sweet fruit of Averrhoa carambola, commonly known as star fruit. Phytochemistry 153, 156â162 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1904">26</a></sup>. Through the ortholog searches in KEGG pathways, we identified enzymes that are potentially involved in the vitamin C and vitamin B<sub>2</sub> biosynthesis pathways in <i>A. carambola</i>.</p><p>In a previous report, a major component of plant ascorbate was reported to be synthesized through the <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wheeler, G. L., Jones, M. A. &amp; Smirnoff, N. The biosynthetic pathway of vitamin C in higher plants. Nature 393, 365 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1919">28</a></sup>, in which GDP-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-mannose is converted to <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-ascorbate through four successive intermediates, as summarized in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig3">3a</a>. Laing et al. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Laing, W. A., Wright, M. A., Cooney, J. &amp; Bulley, S. M. The missing step of the l-galactose pathway of ascorbate biosynthesis in plants, an l-galactose guanyltransferase, increases leaf ascorbate content. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 9534â9539 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d66053e1933">29</a></sup> reported the identification of <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose guanylyltransferase-encoding homologous genes from <i>Arabidopsis</i> and kiwifruit that encode enzymes that catalyze the conversion of GDP-<span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose to <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose-1-P. In this study, five necessary enzymes (GalDH, GalLDH, GGalPP, GalPP, and GME) involved in the vitamin C pathway were identified, suggesting the possibility of ascorbic acid synthesis in star fruit (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Tab3">3</a>). For <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose dehydrogenase (GalDH), we identified four paralogous genes in the star fruit genome. The copy number of GalDH genes in star fruit is close to that in tomato (5) and papaya (4) but approximately half that in other species (10 in poplar, 11 in orange, 8 in Arabidopsis, and 13 in grape, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">12</a>). Further evolutionary analysis showed three clusters in the phylogenetic tree, and the most ancient cluster comprised all the grape genes. Among the other two sister clusters, one is ancient and comes from poplar, including four genes, and the other is closer to orange, including seven genes. The four genes in star fruit are divided into two clusters and were recently separated from their ancestors (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Genes involved in vitamin C metabolism."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Genes involved in vitamin C metabolism.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="495" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> A proposed model for <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-ascorbic acid biosynthesis pathways in star fruit. Genes identified as being involved in the pathways are shown in blue circles. <b>b</b> Phylogenetic analysis of the GalDH gene family in <i>A. carambola</i> (rose red), <i>A. thaliana</i> (purple), <i>C. sinensis</i> (yellow), <i>C. papaya</i> (blue), <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (sky blue), <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (orange), and <i>V. vinifera</i> (green).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 List of genes involved in the vitamin C and vitamin B<sub>2</sub> pathways.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>GalDH</i> <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose dehydrogenase, <i>GalLDH</i> <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactono-1,4-lactone dehydrogenase, <i>GGalPP</i> GDP-<span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose phosphorylase, <i>GalPP</i> <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-galactose-1-phosphate phosphatase, <i>GME</i> GDP-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-mannose-3â²,5â²-epimerase.</p><p>We also identified possible enzymes involved in the riboflavin (vitamin B<sub>2</sub>) biosynthesis pathway in star fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig4">4a</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Tab3">3</a>). Through catalysis by RIB3, RIB4, and RIB5, riboflavin can ultimately be produced from the <span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-ribulose 5-phosphate compound. Furthermore, in the investigation of the possible biosynthesis pathway of the special product oxalate in star fruit, we identified three enzymes, citrate synthase (CS), isocitrate lyase (aceA), and aconitate hydratase (ACO), that can potentially catalyze the transformation of oxalacetic acid to glyoxylate within the glyoxylate and dicarboxylate metabolism pathway (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0306-4/MediaObjects/41438_2020_306_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="264" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Identification of genes involved in the (<b>a</b>) riboflavin and (<b>b</b>) flavonoid biosynthesis pathways. Genes identified as being involved in the two pathways are shown in blue circles. FLAD1 FMN adenylyltransferase, RIB3 3,4-dihydroxy 2-butanone 4-phosphate synthase, RIB4 6,7-dimethyl-8-ribityllumazine synthase, RIB5 riboflavin synthase, RFK riboflavin 5â²-phosphotransferase, ANR anthocyanidin reductase, ANS leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase, CCOAMT caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase, CHI chalcone isomerase, CHS chalcone synthase, CYP75A flavonoid 3â²,5â²-hydroxylase, CYP75B1 flavonoid 3â²-monooxygenase, CYP98A coumaroylquinate 3â²-monooxygenase, DFR flavanone 4-reductase, F3H naringenin 3-dioxygenase, HCT shikimate O-hydroxycinoyltransferase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In the <i>A. carambola</i> gene family analysis, the KEGG pathway enrichment analysis of the expanded gene families revealed that 17 genes participate in the flavonoid synthesis pathway (<i>P</i>-valueâ=â0.03, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Tab3">3</a>). Previous studies have proved that flavonoids can be isolated from <i>A. carambola</i> and other plants from the Oxalidaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muthu, N., Lee, S. Y., Phua, K. K. &amp; Bhore, S. J. Nutritional, medicinal and toxicological attributes of star-fruits (Averrhoa carambola L.): a review. Bioinformation 12, 420â424 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2664">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Suluvoy, J. K., Sakthivel, K. M., Guruvayoorappan, C. &amp; Berlin Grace, V. M. Protective effect of Averrhoa bilimbi L. fruit extract on ulcerative colitis in wistar rats via regulation of inflammatory mediators and cytokines. Biomed. Pharmacother. 91, 1113â1121 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2667">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jia, X., Xie, H., Jiang, Y. &amp; Wei, X. Flavonoids isolated from the fresh sweet fruit of Averrhoa carambola, commonly known as star fruit. Phytochemistry 153, 156â162 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2670">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kurup, S. B. &amp; Mini, S. Averrhoa bilimbi fruits attenuate hyperglycemia-mediated oxidative stress in streptozotocin-induced diabetic rats. J. Food Drug Anal. 25, 360â368 (2017)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2673">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, Y., Zhang, X. &amp; Tian, X. Extraction and purification of flavonoids in Carambola. J. Shenyang Agric. Univ. 40, 491â493 (2009)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2673_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, D., Xie, H., Jia, X. &amp; Wei, X. Flavonoid C-glycosides from star fruit and their antioxidant activity. J. Funct. Foods 16, 204â210 (2015)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2673_2">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Jia, X., Xie, H., Jiang, Y. &amp; Wei, X. Phytochemistry flavonoids isolated from the fresh sweet fruit of Averrhoa carambola, commonly known as star fruit. Phytochem. 153, 156â162 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2676">33</a></sup>. Here, we identified 11 enzymes that could be potentially involved in the flavonoid biosynthesis pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#Fig4">4b</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">11</a>). The two enzymes in the pathway showing the highest copy numbers are shikimate O-hydroxycinnamoyltransferase (HCT) and chalcone synthase (CHS), with 23 and 21 copies, respectively. Among the end-point products, apigenin, cyanidin, epicatechin, and quercetin have been extracted from the leaves, fruits or bark of <i>A. carambola</i> in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Shui, G. &amp; Leong, L. P. Analysis of polyphenolic antioxidants in star fruit using liquid chromatography and mass spectrometry. J. Chromatogr. A 1022, 67â75 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2690">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Moresco, H. H., Queiroz, G. S., Pizzolatti, M. G. &amp; Brighente, I. Chemical constituents and evaluation of the toxic and antioxidant activities of Averrhoa carambola leaves. Rev. Bras. Farmacogn. 22, 319â324 (2012)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2693">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tiwari, K., Masood, M. &amp; Minocha, P. Chemical constituents of Gmelina phillipinensis, Adenocalymna nitida, Allamanda cathartica, Averrhoa carambola and Maba buxifolia. J. Indian Chem. Soc. (1979)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2693_1">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Gunasegaran, R. Flavonoids and anthocyanins of three Oxalidaceae. Fitoterapia 63, 89â90 (1992)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2696">36</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>This study presents the first draft genome in the Oxalidaceae family. The sequenced species, <i>A. carambola</i> (star fruit), is widely cultivated and utilized as an edible fruit and serves as an important source of minerals and vitamins, in addition to presenting phytomedicinal properties. The assembled genome size was 470.51âMb, with a scaffold N50 of 2.76âMb. We cannot compare this genome size with those of other species in this family, but we found similar genome sizes in the closest order Malpighiales of 434.29âMb in <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and 350.62âMb in <i>Ricinus communis</i>. However, the chromosome-level genome will be required to better understand the diploid character of this species.</p><p>In total, 24,726 gene models were identified from <i>A. carambola</i>. This gene number is relatively smaller than those from earlier reports for <i>A. thaliana, P. trichocarpa</i>, or <i>T. cacao</i>. The length distribution of the exons of the predicted star fruit proteins was consistent with those in other species, although the predicted intron and CDS lengths tended to be shorter than those in other species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">3</a>). The proportion of predicted genes containing an InterPro functional domain was 52.3%, and the proportion that could be aligned with the NCBI nr database (66.4%) was the highest among all databases. It is likely that <i>A. carambola</i> is the only species in the Oxalidaceae family whose genome has been assembled so far; there might be some evolutionarily unique genes in this family remaining to be annotated.</p><p>We subsequently performed gene family analysis together with the other 10 species and identified the significant expansion of 888 gene families containing 2916 unique genes in <i>A. carambola</i>. These genes were significantly enriched (<i>P</i>-valueââ¤â0.05) within 28 GO classes, including 18 biological process, 2 cellular component, and 8 molecular function categories (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#MOESM1">6</a>). The biological process of DNA binding contained the most genes (60) within the expanded families. Within the significantly enriched biological processes, the defense response to fungus might be related to the antimicrobial property of star fruits identified in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muthu, N., Lee, S. Y., Phua, K. K. &amp; Bhore, S. J. Nutritional, medicinal and toxicological attributes of star-fruits (Averrhoa carambola L.): a review. Bioinformation 12, 420â424 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2748">1</a></sup>. On the other hand, we found that oxidoreductase activity was enriched in the molecular function GO class, which could be one of the potential reasons for the antioxidant activity of star fruits.</p><p>The genome evolution analysis indicated that star fruit only shared an ancient Î³ event, and no recent WGD was observed. In a genome evolution study of poplar, which belongs to Malpighiales, the existence of a hexaploidization event and recent duplication were reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2755">21</a></sup>. This result could partially explain why star fruit has half the number of gene sets compared to poplar.</p><p>Moreover, among the enriched KEGG pathways, we identified enzymes involved in nutritional metabolic pathways, including the vitamin C, vitamin B<sub>2</sub>, oxalate, and flavonoid pathways. Although potential functional enzymes have been identified from the genome, these functional pathways should be verified by experimental studies in the future.</p><p>In summary, it can be expected that this draft genome will facilitate the elucidation of the development of specific important traits in star fruit plants, such as the biosynthesis pathways of pharmacologically active metabolites, and will contribute to the improvement of breeding strategies for star fruit plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant materials and sequencing</h3><p>Leaf samples of <i>A. carambola</i> were collected from the Ruili Botanical Garden, Yunnan, China. Genomic DNA was extracted by using the cetyl-triethylammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Sahu, S. K., Thangaraj, M. &amp; Kathiresan, K. DNA extraction protocol for plants with high levels of secondary metabolites and polysaccharides without using liquid nitrogen and phenol. ISRN Mol. Biol. 2012, 1â6 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2783">37</a></sup>. 10X Genome sequencing was performed to obtain high-quality reads. High-molecular-weight (HMW) DNA was loaded onto a Chromium Controller chip with 10X Chromium reagents and gel beads, and the rest of the procedures were carried according to the manufacturerâs protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="10X Genomics. &#xA;                  https://www.10xgenomics.com&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2787">38</a></sup>. Then, the BGISEQ-500 platform was used to produce 2âÃâ150âbp paired-end sequences, generating a total of 206.28âGb of raw data. The raw reads were filtered using SOAPfilter v2.2 with the following parameters: â-q 33 -i 600 -p -l -f -z -g 1 -M 2 -Q 20â. After filtering low-quality reads, ~114.42âGb of clean data (high-quality reads &gt;Q35) remained for the next step.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Estimation of <i>A. carambola</i> genome size</h3><p>All 114.42âGb clean reads obtained from the BGISEQ-500 platform were subjected to 17 kmer frequency distribution analysis with Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2803">39</a></sup> using the parameters â-k 17 -t 24â. The frequency graph was drawn, and the <i>A. carambola</i> genome size was calculated using the formula: genome sizeâ=âk-mer_Number/Peak_Depth.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">De novo genome assembly</h3><p>The linked read data were assembled using Supernova v2.1.1 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Weisenfeld, N. I., Kumar, V., Shah, P., Church, D. M. &amp; Jaffe, D. B. Direct determination of diploid genome sequences. Genome Res. 27, 757â767 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2818">13</a></sup> using the â--localcoresâ=â24 --localmem=350 --max reads 280000000â parameter. To fill the scaffold gaps, GapCloser version 1.12<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2822">40</a></sup> was used with the parameters â-t 12 -l 155â. Finally, the total assembled length of the <i>A. carambola</i> genome was 470.51âMb, with a scaffold N50 of 275.76âKb and a contig N50 of 44.84âKb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Repeat annotation</h3><p>For transposable element annotation, RepeatMasker v3.3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="TarailoâGraovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 25, 4.10.11â14.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2837">41</a></sup> and RepeatProteinMasker v3.3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="TarailoâGraovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 25, 4.10.11â14.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2841">41</a></sup> were applied against Repbase v16.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2845">42</a></sup> to identify known repeats in the <i>A. carambola</i> genome. Tandem repeats were identified using Tandem Repeats Finder v4.07b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2852">43</a></sup>. De novo repeat identification was conducted using the RepeatModeler v1.0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2857">44</a></sup> and LTR_FINDER v1.05<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2861">45</a></sup> programs, followed by RepeatMasker v3.3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="TarailoâGraovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 25, 4.10.11â14.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2865">41</a></sup> to obtain the final results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Gene prediction</h3><p>Prior to the gene prediction analysis, we masked the repetitive regions of the genome. MAKER-P v2.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Campbell, M. S., Holt, C., Moore, B. &amp; Yandell, M. Genome annotation and curation using MAKER and MAKERâP. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 48, 4.11.11â14.11.39 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2878">46</a></sup> was utilized to predict protein-coding genes based on homology and de novo prediction evidence. For homology-based prediction, the protein sequences of <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Prunus mume</i>, <i>Prunus avium</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Populus euphratica</i>, and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> were aligned against the <i>A. carambola</i> genome using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2907">47</a></sup>. Then, the gene structure was predicted using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2911">48</a></sup>. To optimize different ab initio gene predictors, we constructed a series of training sets for de novo prediction data. Complete gene models according to homology evidence were picked for training with the Augustus tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Stanke, M., SchÃ¶ffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinform. 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2916">49</a></sup>. Genemark-ES v4.21<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y. O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494â6506 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2920">50</a></sup> was self-trained using the default criteria. The first round of MAKER-P analysis was also run using the default parameters on the basis of the above evidence, with the exception of âprotein2genomeâ, which was set to â1â, yielding only protein-supported gene models. SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinform. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2924">51</a></sup> was then trained with these gene models. The default parameters were used to run the second and final rounds of MAKER-P, generating the final gene models.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Functional annotation</h3><p>The predicted gene models were functionally annotated by aligning their protein sequences against the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2936">52</a></sup>, Clusters of Orthologous Groups (COG)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tatusov, R. L., Galperin, M. Y., Natale, D. A. &amp; Koonin, E. V. The COG database: a tool for genome-scale analysis of protein functions and evolution. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 33â36 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2940">16</a></sup>, SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45â48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2944">17</a></sup>, TrEMBL, and National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) non-redundant (NR) protein databases with BLASTP (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ05). Protein motifs and domains were identified by comparing the sequences against various domain databases, including the PFAM, PRINTS, PANTHER, ProDom, PROSITE, and SMART databases, using InterProScan v5.21<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116âW120 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2951">18</a></sup>. For ncRNA annotation, tRNA genes were identified with tRNAscan-SE v1.23<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2956">53</a></sup>. For the identification of rRNA genes, we aligned the assembled data against the rRNA sequences of <i>A. thaliana</i> using BLASTN (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ05). miRNAs and snRNAs were predicted by using INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335â1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2966">54</a></sup> software against the Rfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Nawrocki, E. P. et al. Rfam 12.0: updates to the RNA families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D130âD137 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d66053e2970">55</a></sup>.</p><p>To classify the NBS domains in the star fruit protein sequences, we used hidden Markov models (HMM) to search for the Pfam NBS (NB-ARC) family PF00931 with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1.0. To detect TIR domains, the amino acid sequences were also screened by using the HMM model Pfam TIR PF01582 (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1.0). Moreover, to identify LRR motifs, we performed an HMM search for Pfam LRR models (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1.0) against star fruit NBS-encoding amino acid sequences.</p><p>To compare the orthologous genes in the vitamin C, vitamin B<sub>2,</sub> flavonoid and oxalate pathways between other plant species (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>), we also annotated the protein sequences by aligning them against the KEGG database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3010">52</a></sup> with BLASTP (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ05) and then performed filtering according to Pfam domains annotated using InterProScan v5.21<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116âW120 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3017">18</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Genome evolution</h3><p>The genome sequences used for orthology analysis were downloaded from NCBI (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>) for <i>A. thaliana</i> (GCA_000001735.2), <i>V. vinifera</i> (GCA_000003745.2), and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (GCA_000002775.3). Next, we used wgd software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Zwaenepoel, A. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. wgdâsimple command line tools for the analysis of ancient whole-genome duplications. Bioinformatics 35, 2153â2155 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3045">56</a></sup> to perform the Ks distribution analysis. The analyses of paralogous gene families and one-to-one orthologs were performed using the âwgd mclâ command. Then, the Ks distribution for a set of paralogous families or one-to-one orthologs was constructed using âwgd ksdâ. Next, we applied the âwgd kdeâ command for the fitting of kernel density estimates (KDEs). Finally, the colinear blocks of the <i>A. carambola</i> paralog were identified by I-ADHoRe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0âfast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3053">57</a></sup> and colored according to their median Ks value.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Gene family construction and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>For gene family analysis, OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3065">23</a></sup> software was utilized to construct the orthologous gene families of all the protein-coding genes of <i>A. carambola</i> and other 10 sequenced plant species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>F. sylvatica</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>K. fedtschenkoi</i>, <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>P. granatum</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>). Before the application of OrthoMCL, BLASTP was used to find similar matches from different species with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1eâ05. The composition of the OrthoMCL clusters was used to calculate the total number of gene families.</p><p>Orthogroups that were present in a single copy in all species analyzed were selected and aligned using MAFFT v7.310<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772â780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3110">58</a></sup>. Each gene tree was constructed by using RAxML v8.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3114">59</a></sup> with the GTRGAMMA model. To construct the species phylogenetic tree, a coalescent-based method in ASTRAL v4.10.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Mirarab, S. et al. ASTRAL: genome-scale coalescent-based species tree estimation. Bioinformatics 30, i541âi548 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3118">60</a></sup> with 100 replicates of multilocus bootstrapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Seo, T.-K. Calculating bootstrap probabilities of phylogeny using multilocus sequence data. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 960â971 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3122">61</a></sup> was used.</p><p>The divergence time between <i>A. carambola</i> and other species was estimated using MCMCTREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3132">59</a></sup> with the default parameters. The expansion and contraction of gene family numbers were predicted using CAFÃ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3136">25</a></sup> by employing the phylogenetic tree and gene family statistics.</p><p>To further perform the phylogenetic analysis of the key enzyme GalDH in the vitamin C pathway, we annotated orthologous genes from six other plant species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>P. trichocarp</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>) using BLASTP with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1eâ05 to align coding sequences against the KEGG database. In total, 55 orthologous genes were used to generate a phylogenetic tree via the maximum likelihood (ML) method in RAxML v 8.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0306-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d66053e3165">59</a></sup>, and 20 runs were included to identify an optimal tree using the GTRGAMMA substitution model and 100 nonparametric bootstrap replicates.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available in the CNGB Nucleotide Sequence Archive (CNSA: <a href="https://db.cngb.org/cnsa">https://db.cngb.org/cnsa</a>). The raw sequencing data are under ID CNR0066625, and assembly data are under ID CNA0002506. All other data generated or analyzed during this study are included in this published article and its supplementary information files.</p>
